<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Abiogenesis</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Natural process by which life arises from non-living matter</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Origin of life" redirects here. For non-scientific views on the origins of life, see <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">Creation myth</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Origin_of_life_stages.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Origin_of_life_stages.svg/400px-Origin_of_life_stages.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Origin_of_life_stages.svg/600px-Origin_of_life_stages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Origin_of_life_stages.svg/800px-Origin_of_life_stages.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Origin_of_life_stages.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stages in the origin of life range from the well-understood, such as the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable Earth</a> and the abiotic synthesis of simple molecules, to the largely unknown, like the derivation of the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA) with its complex molecular functionalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_Packard_Cody_2017_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_Packard_Cody_2017-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <b>abiogenesis</b> (from a-‘not’ + Greek bios ‘life’ + genesis 'origin') or the <b>origin of life</b> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural" title="Natural">natural</a> process by which <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> has arisen from non-living matter, such as simple <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>. The prevailing scientific hypothesis is that the transition from non-living to living entities was not a single event, but an evolutionary process of increasing complexity that involved the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable planet</a>, the prebiotic synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic molecules</a>, molecular <a href="/wiki/Self-replication" title="Self-replication">self-replication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autocatalysis" title="Autocatalysis">autocatalysis</a>, and the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a>. Many proposals have been made for different stages of the process.
</p><p>The study of abiogenesis aims to determine how pre-life <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a> gave rise to life under conditions strikingly different from those on Earth today. It primarily uses tools from biology and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, with more recent approaches attempting a synthesis of many sciences. Life functions through the specialized chemistry of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and water, and builds largely upon four key families of chemicals: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">lipids</a> for cell membranes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> such as sugars, <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> for protein metabolism, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleic_acids" title="Nucleic acids">nucleic acids</a> DNA and RNA for the mechanisms of heredity. Any successful theory of abiogenesis must explain the origins and interactions of these classes of molecules. Many approaches to abiogenesis investigate how <a href="/wiki/Self-replication" title="Self-replication">self-replicating</a> molecules, or their components, came into existence. Researchers generally think that current life descends from an <a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">RNA world</a>, although other self-replicating molecules may have preceded RNA.
</p><p>The classic 1952 <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a> demonstrated that most <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, the chemical constituents of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, can be synthesized from <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a> under conditions intended to replicate those of the <a href="/wiki/Prebiotic_atmosphere" title="Prebiotic atmosphere">early Earth</a>. External sources of energy may have triggered these reactions, including <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>, atmospheric entries of micro-meteorites and implosion of bubbles in sea and ocean waves. Other approaches ("metabolism-first" hypotheses) focus on understanding how <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> in chemical systems on the early Earth might have provided the <a href="/wiki/Precursor_(chemistry)" title="Precursor (chemistry)">precursor molecules</a> necessary for self-replication.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomics</a> approach has sought to characterise the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA) of modern organisms by identifying the genes shared by <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a>, members of the two major branches of life (where the <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">Eukaryotes</a> belong to the archaean branch in the <a href="/wiki/Two-domain_system" title="Two-domain system">two-domain system</a>). 355 genes appear to be common to all life; their nature implies that the LUCA was <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway" title="Wood–Ljungdahl pathway">Wood–Ljungdahl pathway</a>, deriving energy by <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">chemiosmosis</a>, and maintaining its hereditary material with <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>. Although the LUCA lived over 4 billion years ago (4 <a href="/wiki/Billion_years" title="Billion years">Gya</a>), researchers do not believe it was the first form of life. Earlier cells might have had a leaky membrane and been powered by a naturally-occurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_gradient" title="Proton gradient">proton gradient</a> near a deep-sea white smoker <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a>.
</p><p>Earth remains the only place in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> known to harbor life, and <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">fossil evidence from the Earth</a> informs most studies of abiogenesis. The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Earth" title="Age of Earth">Earth</a> was formed 4.54 Gya; the earliest undisputed evidence of life on Earth dates from at least 3.5 Gya. <a href="/wiki/Micropaleontology#Microfossils" title="Micropaleontology">Fossil micro-organisms</a> appear to have lived within hydrothermal vent precipitates dated 3.77 to 4.28 Gya <a href="/wiki/Nuvvuagittuq_Greenstone_Belt" title="Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt">from Quebec</a>, soon after <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">ocean formation</a> 4.4 Gya during the <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">Hadean</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">Astrobiology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_on_astrobiology.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1306" data-file-width="1930" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASA_on_astrobiology.svg/350px-NASA_on_astrobiology.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASA_on_astrobiology.svg/525px-NASA_on_astrobiology.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASA_on_astrobiology.svg/700px-NASA_on_astrobiology.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA_on_astrobiology.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NASA's 2015 strategy for <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> aimed to solve the puzzle of the origin of life – how a fully-functioning living system could emerge from non-living components – through research on the prebiotic origin of <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">life's chemicals</a>, both in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a> and on <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, as well as the functioning of early biomolecules to <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyse</a> reactions and support <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">inheritance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_strategy_2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Life#Definitions" title="Life">Life</a> consists of reproduction with (heritable) variations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBSD-20110317_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-JBSD-20110317-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> defines life as "a self-sustaining chemical system capable of Darwinian evolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20210306_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20210306-4">[4]</a></sup> Such a system is complex; the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA), presumably a single-celled organism which lived some 4 billion years ago, already had hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> encoded in the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> that is universal today. That in turn implies a suite of cellular machinery including <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNAs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> to translate the code into <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>. Those proteins included <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> to operate its <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic respiration</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway" title="Wood–Ljungdahl pathway">Wood–Ljungdahl metabolic pathway</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/DNA_polymerase" title="DNA polymerase">DNA polymerase</a> to replicate its genetic material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzany_2016_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witzany_2016-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AB-20141208_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-AB-20141208-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The challenge for abiogenesis (origin of life)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pereto_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pereto-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20151218_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20151218-9">[9]</a></sup> researchers is to explain how such a complex and tightly-interlinked system could develop by evolutionary steps, as at first sight <a href="/wiki/Irreducible_complexity" title="Irreducible complexity">all its parts are necessary</a> to enable it to function. For example, a cell, whether the LUCA or in a modern organism, copies its DNA with the DNA polymerase enzyme, which is in turn produced by translating the DNA polymerase gene in the DNA. Neither the enzyme nor the DNA can be produced without the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_2016_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss_2016-10">[10]</a></sup> The evolutionary process could have involved molecular <a href="/wiki/Self-replication" title="Self-replication">self-replication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a> such as of <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Autocatalysis" title="Autocatalysis">autocatalysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzany_2016_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Witzany_2016-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AB-20141208_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-AB-20141208-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA-20150420_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-20150420-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The precursors to the development of a living cell like the LUCA are clear enough, if disputed in their details: a habitable world is formed with a supply of minerals and liquid water. Prebiotic synthesis creates a range of simple organic compounds, which are assembled into polymers such as proteins and RNA. The process after the LUCA, too, is readily understood: Darwinian evolution caused the development of a wide range of species with varied forms and biochemical capabilities. The derivation of living things such as the LUCA from simple components, however, is far from understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_Packard_Cody_2017_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_Packard_Cody_2017-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Earth remains the only place where life is known,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-1990_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-1990-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> the science of <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> seeks evidence of life on other planets. The 2015 NASA strategy on the origin of life aimed to solve the puzzle by identifying interactions, intermediary structures and functions, energy sources, and environmental factors that contributed to the diversity, selection, and replication of evolvable macromolecular systems,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_strategy_2015_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-2">[2]</a></sup> and mapping the chemical landscape of potential primordial informational <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>. The advent of polymers that could replicate, store genetic information, and exhibit properties subject to selection was, it suggested, most likely a critical step in the <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">emergence</a> of prebiotic chemical evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_strategy_2015_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Those polymers derived, in turn, from simple <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> that could have been formed by reactions in the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oparin_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oparin-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pereto_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pereto-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> A successful theory of the origin of life must explain how all these chemicals came into being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conceptual_history_until_the_1960s">Conceptual history until the 1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Conceptual history until the 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_research_into_the_origin_of_life" title="History of research into the origin of life">History of research into the origin of life</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="653" decoding="async" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg/440px-Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg/660px-Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg/880px-Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a> was a synthesis of small organic molecules in a mixture of simple gases in a thermal gradient created by heating (right) and cooling (left) the mixture at the same time, with electrical discharges.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spontaneous_generation">Spontaneous generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spontaneous generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">Spontaneous generation</a></div>
<p>One ancient view of the origin of life, from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">Aristotle</a> until the 19th century, is of <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This theory held that "lower" animals were generated by decaying organic substances, and that life arose by chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernal_1967_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernal_1967-20">[20]</a></sup> This was questioned from the 17th century, in works like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Browne" title="Sir Thomas Browne">Thomas Browne</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudodoxia_Epidemica" title="Pseudodoxia Epidemica">Pseudodoxia Epidemica</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1665, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> published the first drawings of a <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganism</a>. In 1676, <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> drew and described microorganisms, probably <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Van Leeuwenhoek disagreed with spontaneous generation, and by the 1680s convinced himself, using experiments ranging from sealed and open meat incubation and the close study of insect reproduction, that the theory was incorrect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1668 <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Redi" title="Francesco Redi">Francesco Redi</a> showed that no <a href="/wiki/Maggot" title="Maggot">maggots</a> appeared in meat when flies were prevented from laying eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev-25">[25]</a></sup> By the middle of the 19th century, spontaneous generation was considered disproven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyndall_Fragments2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyndall_Fragments2-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panspermia">Panspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Panspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Panspermia" title="Panspermia">Panspermia</a></div>
<p>Another ancient idea dating back to <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a> in the 5th century BC is <a href="/wiki/Panspermia" title="Panspermia">panspermia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerda_Horneck_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerda_Horneck-28">[28]</a></sup> the idea that <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> exists throughout the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, distributed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteoroids" title="Meteoroids">meteoroids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cometary_panspermia_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-cometary_panspermia-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">planetoids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> It does not attempt to explain how life originated in itself, but shifts the origin of life on Earth to another heavenly body. The advantage is that life is not required to have formed on each planet it occurs on, but rather in a more limited set of locations (potentially even a single location), and then spread about the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> to other star systems via cometary or meteorite impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160912_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160912-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".22A_warm_little_pond.22:_primordial_soup"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"A_warm_little_pond":_primordial_soup'>"A warm little pond": primordial soup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title='Edit section: "A warm little pond": primordial soup'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Primordial_soup" title="Primordial soup">Primordial soup</a></div>
<p>The idea that life originated from non-living matter in slow stages appeared in <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a>'s 1864–1867 book <i>Principles of Biology</i>, and in <a href="/wiki/William_Turner_Thiselton-Dyer" title="William Turner Thiselton-Dyer">William Turner Thiselton-Dyer</a>'s 1879 paper "On spontaneous generation and evolution". On 1 February 1871 <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> wrote about these publications to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dalton_Hooker" title="Joseph Dalton Hooker">Joseph Hooker</a>, and set out his own speculation, suggesting that the original spark of life may have begun in a "warm little pond, with all sorts of ammonia and phosphoric salts, light, heat, electricity, &amp;c., present, that a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteine</a> compound was chemically formed ready to undergo still more complex changes." Darwin went on to explain that "at the present day such matter would be instantly devoured or absorbed, which would not have been the case before living creatures were formed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darwin_DCP-LETT-7471_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Darwin_DCP-LETT-7471-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20201111_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20201111-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Oparin" title="Alexander Oparin">Alexander Oparin</a> in 1924 and <a href="/wiki/J._B._S._Haldane" title="J. B. S. Haldane">J. B. S. Haldane</a> in 1929 proposed that the first molecules constituting the earliest cells slowly self-organized from a <a href="/wiki/Primordial_soup" title="Primordial soup">primordial soup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bah2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-bah2-35">[35]</a></sup> Haldane suggested that the Earth's prebiotic oceans consisted of a "hot dilute soup" in which organic compounds could have formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernal_1967_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernal_1967-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._D._Bernal" title="J. D. Bernal">J. D. Bernal</a> showed that such mechanisms could form most of the necessary molecules for life from inorganic precursors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1967, he suggested three "stages": the origin of biological <a href="/wiki/Monomer" title="Monomer">monomers</a>; the origin of biological polymers; and the evolution from molecules to cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Miller.E2.80.93Urey_experiment"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Miller–Urey_experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Miller–Urey experiment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a></div>
<p>In 1952, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanley_L._Miller" title="Stanley L. Miller">Stanley Miller</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harold_C._Urey" title="Harold C. Urey">Harold Urey</a> carried out a chemical experiment to demonstrate how organic molecules could have formed spontaneously from inorganic precursors under <a href="/wiki/Prebiotic_atmosphere" title="Prebiotic atmosphere">prebiotic conditions</a> like those posited by the Oparin-Haldane hypothesis. It used a highly <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> (lacking oxygen) mixture of gases—<a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_gas" title="Hydrogen gas">hydrogen</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>—to form simple organic monomers such as amino acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21422282_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21422282-41">[41]</a></sup> Bernal said of the Miller–Urey experiment that "it is not enough to explain the formation of such molecules, what is necessary, is a physical-chemical explanation of the origins of these molecules that suggests the presence of suitable sources and sinks for free energy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> However, current scientific consensus describes the primitive atmosphere as weakly reducing or neutral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleaves_2008_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cleaves_2008-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chyba_2005_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chyba_2005-44">[44]</a></sup> diminishing the amount and variety of amino acids that could be produced. The addition of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> minerals, present in early oceans, however produces a diverse array of amino acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleaves_2008_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cleaves_2008-43">[43]</a></sup> Later work has focused on two other potential reducing environments: <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> and deep-sea hydrothermal vents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bada_2003_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bada_2003-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_a_habitable_Earth">Producing a habitable Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Producing a habitable Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="nomobile toccolours searchaux" id="Container" role="presentation" style="text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em;border-style:solid;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.3em 0 0.8em style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:23.57em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:21.76em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">−6 —</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:19.95em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:18.13em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">−5 —</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:16.32em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:14.51em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">−4 —</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:12.69em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:10.88em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">−3 —</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:9.07em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:7.25em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">−2 —</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:5.44em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:3.63em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">−1 —</span></div><div style="float:right;position:absolute;right:-1px;top:1.81em;transform:translateY(-50%)"><span style="font-size:90%">–</span></div><div <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_universe_with_first_stars">Early universe with first stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early universe with first stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">Chronology of the universe</a></div>
<p>Soon after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, which occurred roughly 14 Gya, the only chemical elements present in the universe were hydrogen, helium, and lithium, the three lightest atoms in the periodic table. These elements gradually came together to form stars. These early stars were massive and short-lived, producing all the heavier elements through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">Carbon</a>, currently the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">fourth most abundant chemical element</a> in the universe (after <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>), was formed mainly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_dwarf_stars" title="White dwarf stars">white dwarf stars</a>, particularly those bigger than twice the mass of the sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA-20200706_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NA-20200706-48">[48]</a></sup> As these stars reached the end of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_life_cycle" title="Stellar life cycle">lifecycles</a>, they ejected these heavier elements, among them carbon and oxygen, throughout the universe. These heavier elements allowed for the formation of new objects, including rocky planets and other bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">nebular hypothesis</a>, the formation and evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> began 4.6 Gya with the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of a small part of a giant <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a>. Most of the collapsing mass collected in the center, forming the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, while the rest flattened into a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a> out of which the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a> formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_Earth">Emergence of Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Emergence of Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">Geological history of Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">Circumstellar habitable zone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prebiotic_atmosphere" title="Prebiotic atmosphere">Prebiotic atmosphere</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">Earth</a> was formed 4.54 Gya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS1997_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1997-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">Hadean</a> Earth (from its formation until 4 Gya) was at first inhospitable to any living organisms. During its formation, the Earth lost a significant part of its initial mass, and consequentially lacked the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> to hold molecular hydrogen and the bulk of the original inert gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The atmosphere consisted largely of water vapor, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, with smaller amounts of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The solution of carbon dioxide in water is thought to have made the seas slightly <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidic</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of about 5.5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The Hadean atmosphere has been characterized as a "gigantic, productive outdoor chemical laboratory,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup> similar to volcanic gases today which still support some abiotic chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">Oceans</a> may have <a href="/wiki/Cool_early_Earth" title="Cool early Earth">appeared</a> as soon as 200 million years after the Earth formed, in a near-boiling (100 C) reducing environment, as the pH of 5.8 rose rapidly towards neutral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> This scenario has found support from the dating of 4.404 Gya <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">zircon</a> crystals from metamorphosed <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">quartzite</a> of <a href="/wiki/Narryer_Gneiss_Terrane" title="Narryer Gneiss Terrane">Mount Narryer</a> in Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilde_2001_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilde_2001-58">[58]</a></sup> Despite the likely increased volcanism, the Earth may have been a water world between 4.4 and 4.3 Gya, with little if any continental crust, a turbulent atmosphere, and a <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a> subject to intense <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light from a <a href="/wiki/T_Tauri_star" title="T Tauri star">T Tauri stage Sun</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic radiation</a>, and from continued <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rise.2006_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-rise.2006-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> hypothesis posits that the Hadean environment between 4.28<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170301_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170301-60">[60]</a></sup> and 3.8 Gya was highly hazardous to life. Following the <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a>, changes in the orbits of the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a> may have bombarded the Earth with asteroids and comets that pockmarked the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_planets" title="Inner planets">inner planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Frequent collisions would have made <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> unviable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The periods between such devastating events give time windows for the possible origin of life in early environments. If the deep marine hydrothermal setting was the site for the origin of life, then abiogenesis could have happened as early as 4.0-4.2 Gya. If the site was at the surface of the Earth, abiogenesis could have occurred only between 3.7 and 4.0 Gya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> However, new lunar surveys and samples have led scientists, including an architect of the Nice model, to deemphasize the significance of the Late Heavy Bombardment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>If life evolved in the ocean at depths of more than ten meters, it would have been shielded both from late impacts and the then high levels of ultraviolet radiation from the sun. Geothermically heated oceanic crust could have yielded far more organic compounds through deep <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiments</a> indicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The available energy is maximized at 100–150 °C, the temperatures at which <a href="/wiki/Hyperthermophile" title="Hyperthermophile">hyperthermophilic</a> bacteria and <a href="/wiki/Thermoacidophile" title="Thermoacidophile">thermoacidophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> live. These modern organisms may be among the closest surviving relatives of the LUCA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_evidence_of_life">Earliest evidence of life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Earliest evidence of life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">Earliest known life forms</a></div>
<p>Life existed on Earth more than 3.5 Gya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin1-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin2_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-71">[71]</a></sup> during the <a href="/wiki/Eoarchean" title="Eoarchean">Eoarchean</a> when sufficient crust had solidified following the molten Hadean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC-20170509_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-20170509-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-2017_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-2017-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WU-20171218_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-WU-20171218-74">[74]</a></sup> The earliest physical evidence of life so far found consists of <a href="/wiki/Micropaleontology#Microfossils" title="Micropaleontology">microfossils</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nuvvuagittuq_Greenstone_Belt" title="Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt">Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt</a> of Northern Quebec, in <a href="/wiki/Banded_iron_formation" title="Banded iron formation">banded iron formation</a> rocks at least 3.77 and possibly 4.28 Gya. The micro-organisms lived within hydrothermal vent precipitates, soon after the 4.4 Gya <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">formation of oceans</a> during the Hadean. The microbes resembled modern hydrothermal vent bacteria, supporting the view that abiogenesis began in such an environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170301_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170301-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biogenic <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> has been found in 3.7 Gya metasedimentary rocks from southwestern <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-75">[75]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> fossils from 3.49 Gya <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australian</a> sandstone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-76">[76]</a></sup> Evidence of early life in rocks from <a href="/wiki/Akilia" title="Akilia">Akilia</a> Island, near the <a href="/wiki/Isua_Greenstone_Belt" title="Isua Greenstone Belt">Isua supracrustal belt</a> in southwestern Greenland, dating to 3.7 Gya, have shown biogenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_isotope" title="Carbon isotope">carbon isotopes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In other parts of the Isua supracrustal belt, graphite inclusions trapped within <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnet</a> crystals are connected to the other elements of life: oxygen, nitrogen, and possibly phosphorus in the form of <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>, providing further evidence for life 3.7 Gya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pilbara" title="Pilbara">Pilbara</a> region of Western Australia, compelling evidence of early life was found in <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a>-bearing sandstone in a fossilized beach, with rounded tubular cells that <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidized</a> sulfur by photosynthesis in the absence of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">Zircons</a> from Western Australia imply that life existed on Earth at least 4.1 Gya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Pilbara region of Western Australia contains the Dresser Formation with rocks 3.48 Gya, including layered structures called <a href="/wiki/Stromatolite" title="Stromatolite">stromatolites</a>. Their modern counterparts are created by photosynthetic micro-organisms including <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> These lie within undeformed hydrothermal-sedimentary strata; their texture indicates a biogenic origin. Parts of the Dresser formation preserve <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> on land, but other regions seem to have been shallow seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 269.33333333333px"><div style="width: 269.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 267.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stromatolites.jpg" title="Stromatolites in the Siyeh Formation, Glacier National Park, dated 3.5 Gya, placing them among the earliest life-forms."><img alt="Stromatolites in the Siyeh Formation, Glacier National Park, dated 3.5 Gya, placing them among the earliest life-forms." data-file-height="371" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/401px-Stromatolites.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg 1.5x" width="268"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Stromatolite" title="Stromatolite">Stromatolites</a> in the Siyeh Formation, <a href="/wiki/Glacier_National_Park_(U.S.)" title="Glacier National Park (U.S.)">Glacier National Park</a>, dated 3.5 Gya, placing them among the earliest life-forms.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 244px"><div style="width: 244px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 242px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg" title="Modern stromatolites in Shark Bay, created by photosynthetic cyanobacteria."><img alt="Modern stromatolites in Shark Bay, created by photosynthetic cyanobacteria." data-file-height="1816" data-file-width="2440" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg/363px-Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg/544px-Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg/726px-Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg 2x" width="242"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_molecules:_prebiotic_synthesis">Producing molecules: prebiotic synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Producing molecules: prebiotic synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> except for hydrogen and helium derive from <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>. The basic chemical ingredients of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> – the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-hydrogen_bond" title="Carbon-hydrogen bond">carbon-hydrogen molecule</a> (CH), the carbon-hydrogen positive ion (CH+) and the carbon ion (C+) – were produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_light" title="Ultraviolet light">ultraviolet light</a> from stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20161012_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20161012-84">[84]</a></sup> Complex molecules, including organic molecules, form naturally both in space and on planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrenfreund2010_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrenfreund2010-85">[85]</a></sup> Organic molecules on the early Earth could have had either terrestrial origins, with organic molecule synthesis driven by impact shocks or by other energy sources, such as ultraviolet light, <a href="/wiki/Organic_redox_reaction" title="Organic redox reaction">redox</a> coupling, or electrical discharges; or extraterrestrial origins (<a href="/wiki/Panspermia#Origination_and_distribution_of_organic_molecules:_Pseudo-panspermia" title="Panspermia">pseudo-panspermia</a>), with organic molecules formed in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_cloud" title="Interstellar cloud">interstellar dust clouds</a> raining down on to the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_extraterrestrial_organic_molecules">Observed extraterrestrial organic molecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Observed extraterrestrial organic molecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_interstellar_and_circumstellar_molecules" title="List of interstellar and circumstellar molecules">List of interstellar and circumstellar molecules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-panspermia" title="Pseudo-panspermia">Pseudo-panspermia</a></div>
<p>An organic compound is a chemical whose molecules contain carbon. Carbon is abundant in the Sun, stars, comets, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body%27s_atmosphere" title="Celestial body's atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of most planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-88">[88]</a></sup> Organic compounds are relatively common in space, formed by "factories of complex molecular synthesis" which occur in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_envelope" title="Circumstellar envelope">circumstellar envelopes</a>, and chemically evolve after reactions are initiated mostly by <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrenfreund2010_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrenfreund2010-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">Purine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a> <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobases</a> including <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. These could have provided the materials for <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> to form on the <a href="/wiki/Early_Earth" title="Early Earth">early Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC-20220426_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-20220426-91">[91]</a></sup> The amino acid <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> was found in material ejected from comet <a href="/wiki/81P/Wild" title="81P/Wild">Wild 2</a>; it had earlier been detected in meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Comets are encrusted with dark material, thought to be a <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a>-like organic substance formed from simple carbon compounds under ionizing radiation. A rain of material from comets could have brought such complex organic molecules to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup> It is estimated that during the Late Heavy Bombardment, meteorites may have delivered up to five million <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">tons</a> of organic prebiotic elements to Earth per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="PAH_world_hypothesis">PAH world hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: PAH world hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">PAH world hypothesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1848" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg/330px-PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg/495px-PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg/660px-PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cat%27s_Paw_Nebula" title="Cat's Paw Nebula">Cat's Paw Nebula</a> is inside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy" title="Milky Way Galaxy">Milky Way Galaxy</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Scorpius" title="Scorpius">Scorpius</a>.<br/>Green areas show regions where radiation from hot stars collided with large molecules and small dust grains called "<a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a>" (PAHs), causing them to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluoresce" title="Fluoresce">fluoresce</a>. <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a>, 2018</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAH) are the most common and abundant polyatomic molecules in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>, and are a major store of carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20051010_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20051010-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20110413_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20110413-97">[97]</a></sup> They seem to have formed shortly after the Big Bang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20051018_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20051018-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20051010_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20051010-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20110413_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20110413-97">[97]</a></sup> and are associated with <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">new stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-88">[88]</a></sup> They are a likely constituent of Earth's primordial sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20051018_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20051018-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20051010_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20051010-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20110413_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20110413-97">[97]</a></sup> PAHs have been detected in <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJL-20101120_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJL-20101120-99">[99]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>, in comets, and in meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The PAH world hypothesis posits PAHs as precursors to the RNA world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> A star, HH 46-IR, resembling the sun early in its life, is surrounded by a disk of material which contains molecules including cyanide compounds, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, and carbon monoxide. PAHs in the interstellar medium can be transformed through <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygenate" title="Oxygenate">oxygenation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxylation" title="Hydroxylation">hydroxylation</a> to more complex organic compounds used in living cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJL-20120901_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJL-20120901-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleobases">Nucleobases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Nucleobases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The majority of organic compounds introduced on Earth by interstellar dust particles have helped to form complex molecules, thanks to their peculiar <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">surface-catalytic</a> activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lincei_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lincei-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Studies of the <sup>12</sup>C/<sup>13</sup>C <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">isotopic ratios</a> of organic compounds in the Murchison meteorite suggest that the RNA component uracil and related molecules, including <a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">xanthine</a>, were formed extraterrestrially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murch_base_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murch_base-104">[104]</a></sup> NASA studies of meteorites suggest that all four DNA <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobases</a> (adenine, guanine and related organic molecules) have been formed in outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lincei_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lincei-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callahan_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callahan-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steigerwald_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steigerwald-106">[106]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a> permeating the universe contains complex organics ("amorphous organic solids with a mixed <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">aromatic</a>–<a href="/wiki/Aliphatic_compound" title="Aliphatic compound">aliphatic</a> structure") that could be created rapidly by stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20111026_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20111026-107">[107]</a></sup> Glycolaldehyde, a sugar molecule and RNA precursor, has been detected in regions of space including around <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostars</a> and on meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20191113_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20191113-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_synthesis">Laboratory synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Laboratory synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As early as the 1860s, experiments demonstrated that biologically relevant molecules can be produced from interaction of simple carbon sources with abundant inorganic catalysts. The spontaneous formation of complex polymers from abiotically generated monomers under the conditions posited by the "soup" theory is not straightforward. Besides the necessary basic organic monomers, compounds that would have prohibited the formation of polymers were also formed in high concentration during the Miller–Urey and <a href="/wiki/Joan_Or%C3%B3" title="Joan Oró">Joan Oró</a> experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Biology uses essentially 20 amino acids for its coded protein enzymes, representing a very small subset of the structurally possible products. Since life tends to use whatever is available, an explanation is needed for why the set used is so small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sugars">Sugars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sugars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Formose.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2216" data-file-width="2617" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Formose.png/330px-Formose.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Formose.png/495px-Formose.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Formose.png/660px-Formose.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Formose.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Breslow catalytic cycle for formaldehyde dimerization and C2-C6 sugar formation</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Butlerov" title="Alexander Butlerov">Alexander Butlerov</a> showed in 1861 that the <a href="/wiki/Formose_reaction" title="Formose reaction">formose reaction</a> created sugars including tetroses, pentoses, and hexoses when <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> is heated under basic conditions with divalent metal ions like calcium. R. Breslow proposed that the reaction was autocatalytic in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleobases_2">Nucleobases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nucleobases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Nucleobases like guanine and adenine can be synthesized from simple carbon and nitrogen sources like <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> (HCN) and ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Formamide" title="Formamide">Formamide</a> produces all four ribonucleotides when warmed with terrestrial minerals. Formamide is ubiquitous in the Universe, produced by the reaction of water and HCN. It can be concentrated by the evaporation of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladino2012_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladino2012-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladino2012b_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladino2012b-115">[115]</a></sup> HCN is poisonous only to <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> and aerobic bacteria), which did not yet exist. It can play roles in other chemical processes such as the synthesis of the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-5"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>DNA and RNA components including uracil, cytosine and thymine can be synthesized under outer space conditions, using starting chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a> found in meteorites. Pyrimidine may have been formed in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> stars or in interstellar dust and gas clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150303_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150303-116">[116]</a></sup> All four RNA-bases may be synthesized from formamide in high-energy density events like extraterrestrial impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other pathways for synthesizing bases from inorganic materials have been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basile1984_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basile1984-118">[118]</a></sup> Freezing temperatures are advantageous for the synthesis of purines, due to the concentrating effect for key precursors such as hydrogen cyanide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> However, while adenine and <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> require freezing conditions for synthesis, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a> may require boiling temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Seven different amino acids and eleven types of <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobases</a> formed in ice when ammonia and <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> were left in a freezer for 25 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> S-<a href="/wiki/Triazine" title="Triazine">triazines</a> (alternative nucleobases), <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidines</a> including cytosine and uracil, and adenine can be synthesized by subjecting a urea solution to freeze-thaw cycles under a reductive atmosphere, with spark discharges as an energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The explanation given for the unusual speed of these reactions at such a low temperature is <a href="/wiki/Eutectic_system" title="Eutectic system">eutectic freezing</a>, which crowds impurities in microscopic pockets of liquid within the ice, causing the molecules to collide more often.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_suitable_vesicles">Producing suitable vesicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Producing suitable vesicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gard_model" title="Gard model">Gard model</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-organization#Biology" title="Self-organization">Self-organization § Biology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="331" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg/170px-Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg/255px-Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg/340px-Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The three main structures composed of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a> form spontaneously by <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a> in solution: the <a href="/wiki/Liposome" title="Liposome">liposome</a> (a closed bilayer), the <a href="/wiki/Micelle" title="Micelle">micelle</a> and the bilayer.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gard_model" title="Gard model">lipid world</a> theory postulates that the first self-replicating object was <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a>-like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Phospholipids form <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid bilayers</a> in water while under agitation—the same structure as in cell membranes. These molecules were not present on early Earth, but other <a href="/wiki/Amphiphile" title="Amphiphile">amphiphilic</a> long-chain molecules also form membranes. These bodies may expand by insertion of additional lipids, and may spontaneously split into two  <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a> of similar size and composition. The main idea is that the molecular composition of the lipid bodies is a preliminary to information storage, and that evolution led to the appearance of polymers like RNA that store information. Studies on vesicles from amphiphiles that might have existed in the prebiotic world have so far been limited to systems of one or two types of amphiphiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2010_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2010-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>A lipid bilayer membrane could be composed of a huge number of combinations of arrangements of amphiphiles. The best of these would have favored the constitution of a hypercycle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> actually a positive <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">feedback</a> composed of two mutual catalysts represented by a membrane site and a specific compound trapped in the vesicle. Such site/compound pairs are transmissible to the daughter vesicles leading to the emergence of distinct <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineages</a> of vesicles, which would have allowed Darwinian natural selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Protocell" title="Protocell">protocell</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-organized, self-ordered</a>, spherical collection of <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> proposed as a stepping-stone to the origin of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2010_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2010-127">[127]</a></sup> The theory of classical irreversible thermodynamics treats self-assembly under a generalized chemical potential within the framework of <a href="/wiki/Dissipative_system" title="Dissipative system">dissipative systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>A central question in evolution is how simple protocells first arose and differed in reproductive contribution to the following generation, thus driving the evolution of life. A functional protocell has (as of 2014) not yet been achieved in a laboratory setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exploring_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Exploring-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2006_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2006-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discover_2004_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discover_2004-136">[136]</a></sup> Self-assembled <a href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology_and_chemistry)" title="Vesicle (biology and chemistry)">vesicles</a> are essential components of primitive cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2010_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2010-127">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> requires that the universe move in a direction in which <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> increases, yet life is distinguished by its great degree of organization. Therefore, a boundary is needed to separate <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">life processes</a> from non-living matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm_2007_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm_2007-137">[137]</a></sup> Irene Chen and <a href="/wiki/Jack_W._Szostak" title="Jack W. Szostak">Jack W. Szostak</a> suggest that elementary protocells can give rise to cellular behaviors including primitive forms of differential reproduction, competition, and energy storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2006_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2006-135">[135]</a></sup> Competition for membrane molecules would favor stabilized membranes, suggesting a selective advantage for the evolution of cross-linked fatty acids and even the <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a> of today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2006_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2006-135">[135]</a></sup> Such <a href="/wiki/Micro-encapsulation" title="Micro-encapsulation">micro-encapsulation</a> would allow for metabolism within the membrane and the exchange of small molecules, while retaining large biomolecules inside. Such a membrane is needed for a cell to create its own <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradient</a> to store energy by pumping ions across the membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2015_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2015-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
