<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_biology">Producing biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Producing biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_and_entropy">Energy and entropy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Energy and entropy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Life requires a loss of <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>, or disorder, when molecules organize themselves into living matter. The emergence of life and increased complexity does not contradict the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>, which states that overall entropy never decreases, since a living organism creates order in some places (e.g. its living body) at the expense of an increase of entropy elsewhere (e.g. heat and waste production).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-2018_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-2018-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Multiple sources of energy were available for chemical reactions on the early Earth. Heat from <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal</a> processes is a standard energy source for chemistry. Other examples include sunlight, lightning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-6"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup> atmospheric entries of micro-meteorites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bar-Nun_Bar-Nun_Bauer_Sagan_1970_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bar-Nun_Bar-Nun_Bauer_Sagan_1970-143">[143]</a></sup> and implosion of bubbles in sea and ocean waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anbar_1968_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anbar_1968-144">[144]</a></sup> This has been confirmed by experiments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dharmarathne_Grieser_2016_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmarathne_Grieser_2016-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patehebieke_Zhao_Wang_Xu_2021_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patehebieke_Zhao_Wang_Xu_2021-146">[146]</a></sup> and simulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalson_Furman_Zeiri_2017_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalson_Furman_Zeiri_2017-147">[147]</a></sup>
Unfavorable reactions can be driven by highly favorable ones, as in the case of iron-sulfur chemistry. For example, this was probably important for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[a]</a></sup> Carbon fixation by reaction of CO<sub>2</sub> with H<sub>2</sub>S via iron-sulfur chemistry is favorable, and occurs at neutral pH and 100 °C. Iron-sulfur surfaces, which are abundant near hydrothermal vents, can drive the production of small amounts of amino acids and other biomolecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Follmann2009_56-7"><a href="#cite_note-Follmann2009-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemiosmosis">Chemiosmosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Chemiosmosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">Chemiosmosis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ATP-Synthase.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="894" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/ATP-Synthase.svg/170px-ATP-Synthase.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/ATP-Synthase.svg/255px-ATP-Synthase.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/ATP-Synthase.svg/340px-ATP-Synthase.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ATP-Synthase.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a> uses the chemiosmotic proton gradient to power ATP synthesis through <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1961, <a href="/wiki/Peter_D._Mitchell" title="Peter D. Mitchell">Peter Mitchell</a> proposed <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">chemiosmosis</a> as a cell's primary system of energy conversion. The mechanism, now ubiquitous in living cells, powers energy conversion in micro-organisms and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> of eukaryotes, making it a likely candidate for early life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> Mitochondria produce <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the energy currency of the cell used to drive cellular processes such as chemical syntheses. The mechanism of ATP synthesis involves a closed membrane in which the <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a> enzyme is embedded. The energy required to release strongly-bound ATP has its origin in <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> that move across the membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junge_Nelson_2015_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Junge_Nelson_2015-151">[150]</a></sup> In modern cells, those proton movements are caused by the pumping of ions across the membrane, maintaining an electrochemical gradient. In the first organisms, the gradient could have been provided by the difference in chemical composition between the flow from a <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a> and the surrounding seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2015_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2015-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg/480px-Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg/720px-Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg/960px-Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg.png 2x" width="480"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">Chemiosmotic</a> coupling in the membranes of a <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrion</a></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_RNA_world">The RNA world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The RNA world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">RNA world</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">RNA world</a> hypothesis describes an early Earth with self-replicating and catalytic RNA but no DNA or proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> Many researchers concur that an RNA world must have preceded the DNA-based life that now dominates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNA_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNA-153">[152]</a></sup> However, RNA-based life may not have been the first to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson2012_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson2012-154">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cech2012_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cech2012-155">[154]</a></sup> Another model echoes Darwin's "warm little pond" with cycles of wetting and drying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>RNA is central to the translation process. Small RNAs can catalyze all the chemical groups and information transfers required for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cech2012_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cech2012-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yarus2011_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yarus2011-157">[156]</a></sup> RNA both expresses and maintains genetic information in modern organisms; and the chemical components of RNA are easily synthesized under the conditions that approximated the early Earth, which were very different from those that prevail today. The structure of the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribozyme</a> has been called the "smoking gun", with a central core of RNA and no amino acid side chains within 18 <a href="/wiki/Angstrom" title="Angstrom">Å</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Active_site" title="Active site">active site</a> that catalyzes peptide bond formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson2012_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson2012-154">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of the RNA world was proposed in 1962 by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Rich" title="Alexander Rich">Alexander Rich</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> and the term was coined by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Gilbert" title="Walter Gilbert">Walter Gilbert</a> in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cech2012_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cech2012-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> There were initial difficulties in the explanation of the abiotic synthesis of the nucleotides <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> Subsequent research has shown possible routes of synthesis; for example, formamide produces all four <a href="/wiki/Ribonucleotide" title="Ribonucleotide">ribonucleotides</a> and other biological molecules when warmed in the presence of various terrestrial minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladino2012_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saladino2012-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladino2012b_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saladino2012b-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Etls-2019-0024c.01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1277" data-file-width="1822" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Etls-2019-0024c.01.png/480px-Etls-2019-0024c.01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Etls-2019-0024c.01.png/720px-Etls-2019-0024c.01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Etls-2019-0024c.01.png/960px-Etls-2019-0024c.01.png 2x" width="480"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Etls-2019-0024c.01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The RNA world hypothesis proposes that undirected polymerisation led to the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a>, and in turn to an <a href="/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="RNA-dependent RNA polymerase">RNA replicase</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="RNA-dependent RNA polymerase">RNA replicase</a> can function as both code and catalyst for further RNA replication, i.e. it can be <a href="/wiki/Autocatalysis" title="Autocatalysis">autocatalytic</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jack_Szostak" title="Jack Szostak">Jack Szostak</a> has shown that certain catalytic RNAs can join smaller RNA sequences together, creating the potential for self-replication. The RNA replication systems, which include two ribozymes that catalyze each other's synthesis, showed a doubling time of the product of about one hour, and were subject to Darwinian <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> under the experimental conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joyce2009_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joyce2009-164">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson2012_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson2012-154">[153]</a></sup> If such conditions were present on early Earth, then natural selection would favor the proliferation of such <a href="/wiki/Autocatalytic_set" title="Autocatalytic set">autocatalytic sets</a>, to which further functionalities could be added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein-166">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michod_1999_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michod_1999-167">[166]</a></sup> Self-assembly of RNA may occur spontaneously in hydrothermal vents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16044244_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16044244-169">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> A preliminary form of <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">tRNA</a> could have assembled into such a replicator molecule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EL-20210302_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-EL-20210302-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Possible precursors to protein synthesis include the synthesis of short peptide cofactors or the self-catalysing duplication of RNA. It is likely that the ancestral ribosome was composed entirely of RNA, although some roles have since been taken over by proteins. Major remaining questions on this topic include identifying the selective force for the evolution of the ribosome and determining how the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> arose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noller2012_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noller2012-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eugene_Koonin" title="Eugene Koonin">Eugene Koonin</a> has argued that "no compelling scenarios currently exist for the origin of replication and translation, the key processes that together comprise the core of biological systems and the apparent pre-requisite of biological evolution. The RNA World concept might offer the best chance for the resolution of this conundrum but so far cannot adequately account for the emergence of an efficient RNA replicase or the translation system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmc1892545_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmc1892545-173">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny_and_LUCA">Phylogeny and LUCA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Phylogeny and LUCA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">Last universal common ancestor</a></div>
<p>Starting with the work of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">Carl Woese</a> from 1977 onwards, <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomics</a> studies have placed the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all modern life-forms between <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> and a clade formed by <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a> in the phylogenetic tree of life. It lived over 4 Gya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese_Fox_1977_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woese_Fox_1977-175">[174]</a></sup> A minority of studies have placed the LUCA in Bacteria, proposing that Archaea and Eukaryota are evolutionarily derived from within Eubacteria;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith" title="Thomas Cavalier-Smith">Thomas Cavalier-Smith</a> suggested that the phenotypically diverse bacterial phylum <a href="/wiki/Chloroflexota" title="Chloroflexota">Chloroflexota</a> contained the LUCA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS2_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-CS2-177">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 370.66666666667px"><div style="width: 370.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 368.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg" title="Phylogenetic tree showing the last universal common ancestor (LUCA) at the root. The major clades are the Bacteria on one hand, and the Archaea and Eukaryota on the other."><img alt="Phylogenetic tree showing the last universal common ancestor (LUCA) at the root. The major clades are the Bacteria on one hand, and the Archaea and Eukaryota on the other." data-file-height="1010" data-file-width="1860" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg/553px-Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg/829px-Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg/1105px-Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg.png 2x" width="369"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA) at the root. The major clades are the <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> on one hand, and the <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a> on the other.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In 2016, a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> likely present in the LUCA was identified. A total of 6.1 million prokaryotic genes from Bacteria and Archaea were sequenced, identifying 355 protein clusters from amongst 286,514 protein clusters that were probably common to the LUCA. The results suggest that the LUCA was <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway" title="Wood–Ljungdahl pathway">Wood–Ljungdahl pathway</a>, nitrogen- and carbon-fixing, thermophilic. Its <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactors</a> suggest dependence upon an environment rich in <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, carbon dioxide, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transition_metals" title="Transition metals">transition metals</a>. Its genetic material was probably DNA, requiring the 4-nucleotide <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNAs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> to translate the code into <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. LUCA likely inhabited an anaerobic <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a> setting in a geochemically active environment. It was evidently already a complex organism, and must have had precursors; it was not the first living thing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_2016_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss_2016-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_2016_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_2016-178">[177]</a></sup> The physiology of LUCA has been in dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 416.66666666667px"><div style="width: 416.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 414.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg" title="LUCA systems and environment[10]"><img alt="LUCA systems and environment[10]" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="1008" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg/622px-LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg/932px-LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg/1243px-LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg.png 2x" width="415"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>LUCA systems and environment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_2016_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss_2016-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Leslie Orgel argued that early translation machinery for the genetic code would be susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Error_catastrophe" title="Error catastrophe">error catastrophe</a>. Geoffrey Hoffmann however showed that such machinery can be stable in function against "Orgel's paradox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Suitable_geological_environments">Suitable geological environments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Suitable geological environments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_abiogenesis_scenarios" title="Alternative abiogenesis scenarios">Alternative abiogenesis scenarios</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_sea_hydrothermal_vents">Deep sea hydrothermal vents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Deep sea hydrothermal vents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">Hydrothermal vent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis#Emergence_of_chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">Chemiosmosis § Emergence of chemiosmosis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg/330px-Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg/495px-Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg/660px-Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">earliest known life forms</a> are putative fossilized <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, found in white smoker <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a> precipitates. They may have lived as early as 4.28 Gya (billion years ago), relatively soon after the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">formation of the oceans</a> 4.41 Gya, not long after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formation of the Earth</a> 4.54 Gya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170301_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170301-60">[60]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Early micro-fossils may have come from a hot world of gases such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulphide" title="Hydrogen sulphide">hydrogen sulphide</a>, toxic to much current life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> Analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_life_(biology)" title="Tree of life (biology)">tree of life</a> places thermophilic and hyperthermophilic <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> closest to the root, suggesting that life may have evolved in a hot environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> The deep sea or alkaline <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a> theory posits that life began at submarine hydrothermal vents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colín-García_2016_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colín-García_2016-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydrothermal_vents_NASA_2014_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-hydrothermal_vents_NASA_2014-188">[187]</a></sup> Martin and Russell have suggested "that life evolved in structured iron monosulphide precipitates in a seepage site hydrothermal mound at a redox, pH, and temperature gradient between sulphide-rich hydrothermal fluid and iron(II)-containing waters of the Hadean ocean floor. The naturally arising, three-dimensional compartmentation observed within fossilized seepage-site metal sulphide precipitates indicates that these inorganic compartments were the precursors of cell walls and membranes found in free-living prokaryotes. The known capability of FeS and NiS to catalyze the synthesis of the acetyl-methylsulphide from carbon monoxide and methylsulphide, constituents of hydrothermal fluid, indicates that pre-biotic syntheses occurred at the inner surfaces of these metal-sulphide-walled compartments".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin2003_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2003-189">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>These form where hydrogen-rich fluids emerge from below the sea floor, as a result of <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">serpentinization</a> of ultra-<a href="/wiki/Mafic" title="Mafic">mafic</a> <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> with seawater and a pH interface with carbon dioxide-rich ocean water. The vents form a sustained chemical energy source derived from redox reactions, in which electron donors (molecular hydrogen) react with electron acceptors (carbon dioxide); see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_world_theory" title="Iron–sulfur world theory">Iron–sulfur world theory</a>. These are <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colín-García_2016_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Colín-García_2016-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[b]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg/440px-Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg/660px-Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg/880px-Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early cell powered by external proton gradient near a deep-sea hydrothermal vent. As long as the membrane (or passive ion channels within it) is permeable to protons, the mechanism can function without ion pumps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2015_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2015-139">[139]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Russell demonstrated that alkaline vents created an abiogenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_electromotive_force" title="Proton electromotive force">proton motive force</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">chemiosmotic</a> gradient,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin2003_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2003-189">[188]</a></sup> ideal for abiogenesis. Their microscopic compartments "provide a natural means of concentrating organic molecules," composed of iron-sulfur minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Mackinawite" title="Mackinawite">mackinawite</a>, endowed these mineral cells with the catalytic properties envisaged by <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_W%C3%A4chtersh%C3%A4user" title="Günter Wächtershäuser">Günter Wächtershäuser</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2009_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2009-191">[189]</a></sup> This movement of ions across the membrane depends on a combination of two factors:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">Diffusion</a> force caused by concentration gradient—all particles including ions tend to diffuse from higher concentration to lower.</li>
<li>Electrostatic force caused by electrical potential gradient—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cations" title="Cations">cations</a> like <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> H<sup>+</sup> tend to diffuse down the electrical potential, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anions" title="Anions">anions</a> in the opposite direction.</li></ol>
<p>These two gradients taken together can be expressed as an <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradient</a>, providing energy for abiogenic synthesis. The proton motive force can be described as the measure of the potential energy stored as a combination of proton and voltage gradients across a membrane (differences in proton concentration and electrical potential).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2015_139-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2015-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>The surfaces of mineral particles inside deep-ocean hydrothermal vents have catalytic properties similar to those of enzymes and can create simple organic molecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> (CH<sub>3</sub>OH), <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyruvic_acid" title="Pyruvic acid">pyruvic acids</a> out of the dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> in the water, if driven by an applied voltage or by reaction with H<sub>2</sub> or H<sub>2</sub>S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organics_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-organics-192">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>The research reported by Martin in 2016 supports the thesis that life arose at hydrothermal vents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> that spontaneous chemistry in the Earth's crust driven by rock–water interactions at disequilibrium thermodynamically underpinned life's origin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup> and that the founding lineages of the archaea and bacteria were H<sub>2</sub>-dependent autotrophs that used CO<sub>2</sub> as their terminal acceptor in energy metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> Martin suggests, based upon this evidence, that the LUCA "may have depended heavily on the geothermal energy of the vent to survive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_Sousa_2016_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss_Sousa_2016-199">[197]</a></sup> Colín-García and colleagues argue that exergonic reactions in hydrothermal vents could provide free energy to promote chemical reactions, and many different minerals. These induce chemical gradients, favoring the interaction between electron donors and acceptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hot_springs">Hot springs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Hot springs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mulkidjanian and co-authors think that the marine environments did not provide the ionic balance and composition universally found in cells, or the ions required by essential proteins and ribozymes found in virtually all living organisms, especially with respect to K<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ratio, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup> and phosphate concentrations. They argue that the only environments that mimic the needed conditions on Earth are hot springs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup> Mineral deposits in these environments under an anoxic atmosphere would have suitable pH (while current pools in an oxygenated atmosphere would not), contain precipitates of sulfide minerals that absorb harmful ultraviolet radiation, have wetting/drying cycles that concentrate substrate solutions to concentrations amenable to spontaneous formation of polymers of nucleic acids, polyesters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup> and depsipeptides,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> created both by chemical reactions in the hydrothermal environment, and by exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_light" title="UV light">UV light</a> during transport from vents to adjacent pools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup> The hypothesized pre-biotic environments are similar to deep-oceanic vent environments, with additional components that help explain peculiarities of the LUCA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> Experimental research at hot springs supports this hypothesis, as RNA-like polymers were synthesized after multiple wet-dry cycles and exposure to UV light. These polymers became encapsulated in vesicles in a moist gel phase, which would not happen in saltwater conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clay">Clay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Clay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The clay hypothesis was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Graham_Cairns-Smith" title="Graham Cairns-Smith">Graham Cairns-Smith</a> in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup> It postulates that complex organic molecules arose gradually on pre-existing, non-organic replication surfaces of silicate crystals in contact with an aqueous solution. The <a href="/wiki/Clay_mineral" title="Clay mineral">clay mineral</a> <a href="/wiki/Montmorillonite" title="Montmorillonite">montmorillonite</a> has been shown to <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyze</a> the <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> of <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> in aqueous solution from <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotide</a> monomers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup> and the formation of membranes from lipids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subramaniam_Wan_Gopinath_Stone_2011_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Subramaniam_Wan_Gopinath_Stone_2011-211">[209]</a></sup> In 1998, Hyman Hartman proposed that "the first organisms were self-replicating iron-rich clays which fixed <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> into <a href="/wiki/Oxalic_acid" title="Oxalic acid">oxalic acid</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Dicarboxylic_acid" title="Dicarboxylic acid">dicarboxylic acids</a>. This system of replicating clays and their metabolic <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> then evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> rich region of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotspring" title="Hotspring">hotspring</a> acquiring the ability to <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">fix nitrogen</a>. Finally <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> was incorporated into the evolving system which allowed the synthesis of nucleotides and <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Iron.E2.80.93sulfur_world"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron–sulfur_world">Iron–sulfur world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Iron–sulfur world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_world_hypothesis" title="Iron–sulfur world hypothesis">Iron–sulfur world hypothesis</a></div>
<p>In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_W%C3%A4chtersh%C3%A4user" title="Günter Wächtershäuser">Günter Wächtershäuser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a> postulated the <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_world_hypothesis" title="Iron–sulfur world hypothesis">Iron–sulfur world hypothesis</a> for the evolution of pre-biotic chemical pathways. It traces today's biochemistry to primordial reactions which synthesize organic building blocks from gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup> Wächtershäuser systems have a built-in source of energy: iron <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a>. The energy released by oxidising these metal sulfides can support synthesis of organic molecules. Such systems may have evolved into autocatalytic sets constituting self-replicating, metabolically active entities predating modern life forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ralser_2014_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ralser_2014-215">[213]</a></sup> Experiments with sulfides in an aqueous environment at 100 °C produced a small yield of <a href="/wiki/Dipeptide" title="Dipeptide">dipeptides</a> (0.4% to 12.4%) and a smaller yield of <a href="/wiki/Tripeptide" title="Tripeptide">tripeptides</a> (0.10%). However, under the same conditions, dipeptides were quickly broken down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several models postulate a primitive metabolism, allowing RNA replication to emerge later. The centrality of the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">Krebs cycle</a> (citric acid cycle) to energy production in aerobic organisms, and in drawing in carbon dioxide and hydrogen ions in biosynthesis of complex organic chemicals, suggests that it was one of the first parts of the metabolism to evolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lane_2009_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lane_2009-191">[189]</a></sup> Concordantly, <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemists</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_W._Szostak" title="Jack W. Szostak">Jack W. Szostak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kate_Adamala" title="Kate Adamala">Kate Adamala</a> demonstrated that non-enzymatic RNA replication in primitive protocells is only possibly in the presence of weak cation <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelators</a> like <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a>. This provides further evidence for the central role of citric acid in primordial metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup> 
Russell has proposed that "the purpose of life is to hydrogenate carbon dioxide" (as part of a "metabolism-first," rather than a "genetics-first," scenario).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musser_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Musser-218">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-219">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ralser_2014_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ralser_2014-215">[213]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_England" title="Jeremy England">Jeremy England</a> has argued from general thermodynamic considerations that life was inevitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup> An early version of this idea was Oparin's 1924 proposal for self-replicating vesicles. In the 1980s and 1990s came Wächtershäuser's iron–sulfur world theory and <a href="/wiki/Christian_de_Duve" title="Christian de Duve">Christian de Duve</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Thioester" title="Thioester">thioester</a> models. More abstract and theoretical arguments for metabolism without genes include <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Dyson" title="Freeman Dyson">Freeman Dyson</a>'s mathematical model and <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Kauffman" title="Stuart Kauffman">Stuart Kauffman</a>'s collectively autocatalytic sets in the 1980s. Kauffman's work has been criticized for ignoring the role of energy in driving biochemical reactions in cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ACS_A_cluster_active_site.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="246" data-file-width="546" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/ACS_A_cluster_active_site.jpg/440px-ACS_A_cluster_active_site.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/ACS_A_cluster_active_site.jpg 1.5x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ACS_A_cluster_active_site.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The active site of the <a href="/wiki/CO-methylating_acetyl-CoA_synthase" title="CO-methylating acetyl-CoA synthase">acetyl-CoA synthase</a> enzyme, part of the  <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a> pathway, contains nickel-iron-sulfur clusters.</div></div></div>
<p>A multistep biochemical pathway like the Krebs cycle did not simply self-organize on the surface of a mineral; it must have been preceded by simpler pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway" title="Wood–Ljungdahl pathway">Wood–Ljungdahl pathway</a> is compatible with self-organization on a metal sulfide surface. Its key enzyme unit, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_dehydrogenase" title="Carbon monoxide dehydrogenase">carbon monoxide dehydrogenase</a>/<a href="/wiki/CO-methylating_acetyl-CoA_synthase" title="CO-methylating acetyl-CoA synthase">acetyl-CoA synthase</a>, contains mixed nickel-iron-sulfur clusters in its reaction centers and catalyzes the formation of <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a>. However, prebiotic <a href="/wiki/Thioacetic_acid" title="Thioacetic acid">thiolated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thioester" title="Thioester">thioester</a> compounds are thermodynamically and kinetically unlikely to accumulate in the presumed prebiotic conditions of hydrothermal vents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup> One possibility is that <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">homocysteine</a> may have reacted with <a href="/wiki/Nitrile" title="Nitrile">nitriles</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Strecker_amino_acid_synthesis" title="Strecker amino acid synthesis">Stecker reaction</a>, forming catalytic thiol-rich polypeptides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zinc-world">Zinc-world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Zinc-world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Armen Mulkidjanian's zinc world (Zn-world) hypothesis extends Wächtershäuser's pyrite hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mulkidjanian_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mulkidjanian-225">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup> The Zn-world theory proposes that hydrothermal fluids rich in H<sub>2</sub>S interacting with cold primordial ocean (or Darwin's "warm little pond") water precipitated metal sulfide particles. Oceanic hydrothermal systems have a zonal structure reflected in ancient <a href="/wiki/Volcanogenic_massive_sulfide_ore_deposit" title="Volcanogenic massive sulfide ore deposit">volcanogenic massive sulfide ore deposits</a>. They reach many kilometers in diameter and date back to the <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archean</a>. Most abundant are pyrite (FeS<sub>2</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Chalcopyrite" title="Chalcopyrite">chalcopyrite</a> (CuFeS<sub>2</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> (ZnS), with additions of <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a> (PbS) and <a href="/wiki/Alabandite" title="Alabandite">alabandite</a> (MnS). ZnS and MnS have a unique ability to store radiation energy, e.g. from ultraviolet light. When replicating molecules were originating, the primordial atmospheric pressure was high enough (&gt;100 bar) to precipitate near the Earth's surface, and ultraviolet irradiation was 10 to 100 times more intense than now; hence the photosynthetic properties mediated by ZnS provided the right energy conditions for the synthesis of informational and metabolic molecules and the selection of photostable nucleobases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mulkidjanian_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mulkidjanian-225">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Zn-world theory has been filled out with evidence for the ionic constitution of the interior of the first proto-cells. In 1926, the Canadian biochemist <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Macallum" title="Archibald Macallum">Archibald Macallum</a> noted the resemblance of <a href="/wiki/Body_fluid" title="Body fluid">body fluids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lymph" title="Lymph">lymph</a> to <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> however, the inorganic composition of all <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> differ from that of modern seawater, which led Mulkidjanian and colleagues to reconstruct the "hatcheries" of the first cells combining geochemical analysis with <a href="/wiki/Phylogenomics" title="Phylogenomics">phylogenomic</a> scrutiny of the inorganic ion requirements of modern cells. The authors conclude that ubiquitous, and by inference primordial, proteins and functional systems show affinity to and functional requirement for K<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">[PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. Geochemical reconstruction shows that this ionic composition could not have existed in the ocean but is compatible with inland geothermal systems. In the oxygen-depleted, CO<sub>2</sub>-dominated primordial atmosphere, the chemistry of water condensates near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermy" title="Geothermy">geothermal fields</a> would resemble the internal milieu of modern cells. Therefore, precellular evolution may have taken place in shallow "Darwin ponds" lined with porous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a> mixed with metal sulfides and enriched in K<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, and phosphorus compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Homochirality">Homochirality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Homochirality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Homochirality" title="Homochirality">Homochirality</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2302" data-file-width="1946" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png/220px-Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png/330px-Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png/440px-Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many biomolecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">L-glutamic acid</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Chirality" title="Chirality">asymmetric</a>, and occur in living systems in only one of the two possible forms, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> the left-handed form. Prebiotic chemistry would produce both forms, creating a puzzle for abiogenesis researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plasson2007_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plasson2007-231">[229]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Homochirality is the geometric uniformity of materials composed of <a href="/wiki/Chirality" title="Chirality">chiral</a> (non-mirror-symmetric) units. Living organisms use molecules that have the same chirality (handedness): with almost no exceptions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup> amino acids are left-handed while nucleotides and <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">sugars</a> are right-handed. Chiral molecules can be synthesized, but in the absence of a chiral source or a chiral <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyst</a>, they are formed in a 50/50 (racemic) mixture of both <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">forms</a>. Known mechanisms for the production of non-racemic mixtures from racemic starting materials include: asymmetric physical laws, such as the <a href="/wiki/Electroweak_interaction" title="Electroweak interaction">electroweak interaction</a>; asymmetric environments, such as those caused by <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circularly polarized</a> light, <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz crystals</a>, or the Earth's rotation, <a href="/wiki/Statistical_fluctuations" title="Statistical fluctuations">statistical fluctuations</a> during racemic synthesis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plasson2007_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plasson2007-231">[229]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_symmetry_breaking" title="Spontaneous symmetry breaking">spontaneous symmetry breaking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jafarpour2017_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-jafarpour2017-233">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jafarpour2015_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-jafarpour2015-234">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frank1953_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-frank1953-235">[233]</a></sup> Once established, chirality would be selected for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup> A small bias (<a href="/wiki/Enantiomeric_excess" title="Enantiomeric excess">enantiomeric excess</a>) in the population can be amplified into a large one by <a href="/wiki/Autocatalysis#Asymmetric_autocatalysis" title="Autocatalysis">asymmetric autocatalysis</a>, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Soai_reaction" title="Soai reaction">Soai reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup> In asymmetric autocatalysis, the catalyst is a chiral molecule, which means that a chiral molecule is catalyzing its own production. An initial enantiomeric excess, such as can be produced by polarized light, then allows the more abundant enantiomer to outcompete the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soai2001_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soai2001-238">[236]</a></sup> Homochirality may have started in outer space, as on the <a href="/wiki/Murchison_meteorite" title="Murchison meteorite">Murchison meteorite</a> the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Alanine" title="Alanine">L-alanine</a> is more than twice as frequent as its D form, and <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">L-glutamic acid</a> is more than three times as abundant as its D counterpart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meierhenrich_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meierhenrich-240">[238]</a></sup> Amino acids from meteorites show a left-handed bias, whereas sugars show a predominantly right-handed bias, as found in living organisms, suggesting an abiogenic origin of these compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StarStuff_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-StarStuff-241">[239]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Formamide-based_prebiotic_chemistry" title="Formamide-based prebiotic chemistry">Formamide-based prebiotic chemistry</a> – Scientific efforts aimed at reconstructing the beginnings of life</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/GADV-protein_world_hypothesis" title="GADV-protein world hypothesis">GADV-protein world hypothesis</a> – A hypothetical stage of abiogenesis</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">Genetic recombination</a> – Production of offspring with combinations of traits that differ from those found in either parent</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shadow_biosphere" title="Shadow biosphere">Shadow biosphere</a> – Hypothetical biosphere of Earth</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explanatory_footnotes">Explanatory footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Explanatory footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The reactions are:
<dl><dd>FeS + H<sub>2</sub>S → FeS<sub>2</sub> + 2H<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>−</sup></dd>
<dd>FeS + H<sub>2</sub>S + CO<sub>2</sub> → FeS<sub>2</sub> + HCOOH</dd></dl>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-190"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-190">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The reactions are:<br/>
<b>Reaction 1</b>: Fayalite + water → magnetite + aqueous silica + hydrogen
<dl><dd>3Fe<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> + 2H<sub>2</sub>O → 2Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 3SiO<sub>2</sub> + 2H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<b>Reaction 2</b>: Forsterite + aqueous silica → serpentine
<dl><dd>3Mg<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> + SiO<sub>2</sub> + 4H<sub>2</sub>O → 2Mg<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<b>Reaction 3</b>: Forsterite + water → serpentine + brucite
<dl><dd>2Mg<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> + 3H<sub>2</sub>O → Mg<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub> + Mg(OH)<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>

Reaction 3 describes the hydration of olivine with water only to yield <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpentine_group" title="Serpentine group">serpentine</a> and Mg(OH)<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Brucite" title="Brucite">brucite</a>). Serpentine is stable at high pH in the presence of brucite like calcium silicate hydrate, (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_silicate_hydrate" title="Calcium silicate hydrate">C-S-H</a>) phases formed along with <a href="/wiki/Portlandite" title="Portlandite">portlandite</a> (Ca(OH)<sub>2</sub>) in hardened <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> paste after the hydration of <a href="/wiki/Belite" title="Belite">belite</a> (Ca<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>), the artificial calcium equivalent of forsterite.

Analogy of reaction 3 with belite hydration in ordinary Portland cement: <i>Belite + water → C-S-H phase + portlandite</i>
<dl><dd>2 Ca<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O → 3 CaO · 2 SiO<sub>2</sub> · 3 H<sub>2</sub>O + Ca(OH)<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
</span></li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-AST-20151218-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AST-20151218_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFScharf2015">Scharf, Caleb;  et al. (18 December 2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4683543" rel="nofollow">"A Strategy for Origins of Life Research"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Astrobiology_(journal)" title="Astrobiology (journal)">Astrobiology</a></i>. <b>15</b> (12): 1031–1042. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2015AsBio..15.1031S" rel="nofollow">2015AsBio..15.1031S</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1089%2Fast.2015.1113" rel="nofollow">10.1089/ast.2015.1113</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4683543" rel="nofollow">4683543</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26684503" rel="nofollow">26684503</a>. <q>What do we mean by the origins of life (OoL)? ... Since the early 20th century the phrase OoL has been used to refer to the events that occurred during the transition from non-living to living systems on Earth, i.e., the origin of terrestrial biology (Oparin, 1924; Haldane, 1929). The term has largely replaced earlier concepts such as abiogenesis (Kamminga, 1980; Fry, 2000).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Astrobiology&amp;rft.atitle=A+Strategy+for+Origins+of+Life+Research&amp;rft.volume=15&amp;rft.issue=12&amp;rft.pages=1031-1042&amp;rft.date=2015-12-18&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4683543%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26684503&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1089%2Fast.2015.1113&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2015AsBio..15.1031S&amp;rft.aulast=Scharf&amp;rft.aufirst=Caleb&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4683543&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbiogenesis"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Weiss_2016-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Weiss_2016_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Weiss_2016_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Weiss_2016_10-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFWeissSousaMrnjavacNeukirchen2016">Weiss, M. C.; Sousa, F. L.; Mrnjavac, N.; Neukirchen, S.; Roettger, M.; Nelson-Sathi, S.; Martin, W.F. (2016). <a class="external text" href="https://www.almendron.com/tribuna/wp-content/uploads/2019/10/the-physiology-and-habitat-of-the-last-universal-common-ancestor.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The physiology and habitat of the last universal common ancestor"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Microbiology" title="Nature Microbiology">Nature Microbiology</a></i>. <b>1</b> (9): 16116. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2FNMICROBIOL.2016.116" rel="nofollow">10.1038/NMICROBIOL.2016.116</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27562259" rel="nofollow">27562259</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:2997255" rel="nofollow">2997255</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature+Microbiology&amp;rft.atitle=The+physiology+and+habitat+of+the+last+universal+common+ancestor&amp;rft.volume=1&amp;rft.issue=9&amp;rft.pages=16116&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A2997255%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F27562259&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2FNMICROBIOL.2016.116&amp;rft.aulast=Weiss&amp;rft.aufirst=M.+C.&amp;rft.au=Sousa%2C+F.+L.&amp;rft.au=Mrnjavac%2C+N.&amp;rft.au=Neukirchen%2C+S.&amp;rft.au=Roettger%2C+M.&amp;rft.au=Nelson-Sathi%2C+S.&amp;rft.au=Martin%2C+W.F.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.almendron.com%2Ftribuna%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F2019%2F10%2Fthe-physiology-and-habitat-of-the-last-universal-common-ancestor.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbiogenesis"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-EA-20150420-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-EA-20150420_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFTirard2015">Tirard, Stephane (20 April 2015). <i>Abiogenesis – Definition</i>. <i>Encyclopedia of Astrobiology</i>. p. 1. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2F978-3-642-27833-4_2-4" rel="nofollow">10.1007/978-3-642-27833-4_2-4</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-642-27833-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-642-27833-4"><bdi>978-3-642-27833-4</bdi></a>. <q>Thomas Huxley (1825–1895) used the term abiogenesis in an important text published in 1870. He strictly made the difference between spontaneous generation, which he did not accept, and the possibility of the evolution of matter from inert to living, without any influence of life. ... Since the end of the nineteenth century, evolutive abiogenesis means increasing complexity and evolution of matter from inert to living state in the abiotic context of evolution of primitive Earth.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Abiogenesis+%E2%80%93+Definition&amp;rft.pages=1&amp;rft.date=2015-04-20&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2F978-3-642-27833-4_2-4&amp;rft.isbn=978-3-642-27833-4&amp;rft.aulast=Tirard&amp;rft.aufirst=Stephane&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbiogenesis"></span></span>
</li>

<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAltermann2009">Altermann, Wladyslaw (2009).
